**Chapter 1: Thirst** The greenhouse stank of rot and promises. Alex squinted through the cracked glass doors, his chef’s knife glinting in the sickly green light filtering through the ruins. The air inside was thick, humid, alive in a way that made his molars ache. He pressed a palm against the doorframe, the rusted metal biting into his skin. “It’s empty,” he called to Jesse, who leaned against the van’s hood, chewing on a strip of dried mutant meat. “Or it was. Ten minutes ago.” Mara stepped around him, her neon-blue hair catching the faint glow of bioluminescent moss crawling up the walls. She crouched to inspect a patch of fungus gnawing through the concrete floor. “Spore residue,” she muttered, swiping a gloved finger through the dust. “Fresh. Whatever died here didn’t do it quietly.” Jesse spat the meat into the dirt. “Great. Dinner’s served. Let’s go.” “*Let’s go*,” Alex repeated, flat. He gestured to the collapsed rafters, where skittering sounds echoed like nails on bone. “You wanna play hero? Fine. But when a clawed shit-goblin jumps out, don’t come crying to me.” “Relax, Grandpa,” Jesse said, though his hands twitched toward the pistol holstered at his thigh. “I’ve seen worse in the Junkyard.” Mara was already inside, her headlamp cutting through the gloom. The greenhouse was a cathedral of decay—shattered glass domed overhead, vines strangling rusted hydroponic tanks. Alex followed, his boots crunching over brittle leaves. The air tasted metallic, wrong. “Look.” Mara pointed to a cluster of mushrooms sprouting from a corpse slumped against a wall. Fat, fleshy caps the color of bruises, glistening with dew that wasn’t there. Alex’s stomach growled. “Poisonous. Probably.” “Probably not,” Mara said, crouching. She pried one free, its stem oozing a viscous yellow fluid. “The spores need a host to activate. These might be dormant.” “Dormant?” Jesse snorted. “You wanna test that theory?” “Someone has to.” Mara’s voice was steady, but her fingers trembled as she tucked the mushroom into her lab coat pocket. “Alex. Your knife.” He tossed it to her. She sliced into the cap, and the smell hit them all at once—earthy and sweet, like overripe apples left to rot in a coffin. Mara held the glistening flesh to her nose. “No hallucinations. Yet.” Alex’s jaw tightened. He remembered his daughter’s laughter, the way it had dissolved into wet coughs as the spores took her. “We’re not eating that.” “Starve, then,” Jesse said, kicking a moldy pot. “I’m not waiting for Preacher’s goons to pick us off like strays.” Mara ignored him. She heated the mushroom in a dented pan over their fire, the spores releasing a haze that shimmered like oil on water. When it was done, she cut a piece and held it out. “Proof.” Alex took a bite. The meat was stringy, bitter, but his stomach stopped growling. He swallowed. “Tastes like ass.” “Good,” Mara said. “Means it’s working.” They ate in silence, the fire crackling. Outside, the wind whistled through broken panes. Alex stared into the flames, carving notches into his knife handle with a bored flick of his wrist. *Three.* His daughter. His wife. The brother who’d dragged him out of the quarantine zone as the walls collapsed. A sound cut through the night—a wet, chittering scrape. Jesse froze. “Did you hear—” The glass door exploded. Something lunged at them, all teeth and jagged bone, its body a grotesque patchwork of fungus and sinew. Mara’s scream merged with Jesse’s wild shot. The creature hit the ground, thrashing, its bioluminescent veins pulsing sickly blue. Alex moved before he thought. He drove his knife into its spine, the blade crunching through brittle cartilage. The thing went still. “Holy shit,” Jesse whispered, staring at the creature’s clawed hand—long, skeletal fingers tipped with hooked talons. “It’s… *pretty*.” Mara crouched beside it, her face pale. “Look at its eyes.” The mutant’s gaze locked onto Alex, pupils dilating in the firelight. For a heartbeat, he swore it *recognized* him. Then its head snapped sideways, jaws unhinging to reveal a spiraling throat lined with spores. Alex recoiled. “We need to burn this. *Now*.” They dragged the body into the woods, where Jesse lit a fire with a flick of his lighter. The flames roared, consuming the mutant’s flesh in a stench of burning hair and acid. Mara watched, her face eerily lit from below. “They’re getting smarter,” she said quietly. Alex said nothing. He stared at the ashes, his daughter’s face flickering in the smoke. *Three.* When they returned to the greenhouse, Jesse lingered by the door, his pistol still drawn. “We’re leaving tomorrow,” he said. “This place is a graveyard.” Mara nodded. “Agreed.” Alex pocketed another mushroom, its cap still warm. *Four.* The night was quiet after that.

**Chapter 2: Hunger** Dawn came gray and thin, slithering through the greenhouse’s shattered windows like a ghost. Alex woke to the sound of Jesse vomiting. “Upchuckin’ mutant guts already?” Alex groaned, rolling onto his back. His daughter’s scarf—it still smelled like her shampoo, impossibly faint—itched against his neck. Jesse hunched over a rusted barrel, his purple hair matted with dirt. “Felt like I swallowed a fucking *firework*.” He wiped his mouth, grimacing. “You see it too, right? The… the *thing*.” Alex sat up. The mushrooms in Mara’s coat pocket pulsed faintly, their caps slick with condensation. “See what?” “The *woman*.” Jesse’s voice dropped. “In the fire. She… she talked.” “Bullshit.” Alex stood, his knees popping. “You ate raw mutant meat. hallucinate ghosts. It’s called *regret*.” Mara emerged from the shadows, her lab coat smeared with ash. She held up a vial of yellow liquid—spore residue scraped from the mutant’s claws. “Regret doesn’t explain this.” Alex didn’t ask. He knew what she’d been doing overnight. The spores were evolving. --- They ate silence for breakfast. Mutant stew—boiled bones, bitter greens, a sprinkle of dried spores for “flavor”—tasted like licking a battery. Mara scribbled in her UV journal by the light of a dying bulb. “More residue in the soil,” she said. “The mushrooms aren’t just dormant. They’re *waiting*.” “Waiting for what?” Jesse stabbed his stew with his knife. “For us to die?” “For the spores to activate,” Mara said. “They’re symbiotic. The mushrooms feed on the mutants, the mutants feed on the spores… and when the spores mature—” “Ain’t gonna happen,” Alex interrupted. “We torch this place and go.” Jesse snorted. “Yeah. Like last time. *Fun* times, huh?” He tapped his temple. “Keep talkin’, Chef. Maybe the voices’ll stop.” A skittering sound cut him off. They froze. Something scraped against the greenhouse’s roof—a rhythmic, deliberate *scratch-scratch-scratch*. “Preacher,” Mara whispered. Alex’s knife was in his hand before the word left her lips. “Stay behind me.” The door creaked open. Sunlight glinted off something pale hanging from the rafters—a shriveled corpse, its skin mottled green, fingers fused into claw-like branches. It swung slowly, a pendulum, as a guttural voice echoed from the trees: *“Repent. Repent. Repent.”* Jesse laughed, high and shaky. “Told you about the voices.” The corpse’s head snapped toward them, jaw unhinging to spill a cloud of spores. Mara hurled her vial. The liquid struck the creature’s chest, sizzling. It writhed, shrieking, as the spores inside devoured it. “Move!” Alex shoved Jesse into the greenhouse as the door exploded. Preacher’s Collective flooded in—six gaunt figures in tattered robes, their flesh bloated with fungal growths. At their center stood Preacher himself, his milky eyes blazing. “Sinner,” he rasped. “You feast on God’s flesh, yet deny Him.” Alex charged, knife raised, but Jesse yanked him back. “They’re *infected*! Don’t touch ’em!” Mara stood frozen, her journal slipping from her hands. The spores drifting from the corpses… they were *different*. Larger. Hungry. One of the Collective lunged, its teeth bared. Alex drove his knife into its throat, but the blade passed through like wet paper. The creature kept coming, its body disintegrating into spores mid-strike, only to reform behind him. “Alex!” Mara screamed. He turned. A spore cloud engulfed him. His scream died as the haze filled his lungs. --- Jesse dragged Alex into the shadows, his hands shaking. “Breathe, breathe, *breathe*—” Mara stared at the spores lingering in the air, their glow now a sickly violet. “They’re… *adapting*,” she breathed. “The more we use them, the smarter they get.” Alex coughed, spitting out a blackened leaf. “Smart enough to kill us.” Preacher’s voice slithered through the greenhouse. “You cannot hide from the Lord’s harvest.” The rafters creaked. More corpses swung down, some humanoid, some… not. One had too many eyes. Another dripped a viscous, glowing sap. Jesse backed toward the door, his pistol trembling. “We need to burn everything. *Now*.” Mara was already at the stove, pouring mutant stew onto the mushrooms. They hissed, smoking, and the greenhouse filled with the stench of burning poison. Alex gripped his knife, his vision swimming. **Chapter 5: Signals** The mushrooms sang. Not with voices, but with light. **Chapter 6: Betrayal** The greenhouse smelled of burnt mushrooms and regret. Alex stirred a pot of stew, the spores in his lungs buzzing like static. Romance: Love in the Limelight. Write a short story set in Wales. Task: write a short story over several chapters. A famous (fictional) actor ducks into a small-town bookstore to escape paparazzi. Romance: Love in the Limelight. Write a short story set in Wales. Task: write a short story over several chapters. A famous (fictional) actor ducks into a small-town bookstore to escape paparazzi. The story takes us through the characters meeting in this odd circumstance. # Brainstorming ## **Characters** 1. **The Actor (Elias "Eli" Hart)** - **Backstory**: Former child star turned A-lister, known for his roguish charm and public scandals. Struggling with fame’s loneliness, he craves validation but fears rejection. - **Personality**: Charismatic, witty, but deeply insecure. Uses humor to deflect vulnerability. Flips between confidence and self-doubt. - **Motivation**: Escape paparazzi, reconnect with simplicity, and subtly test if anyone sees *him* beyond his fame. 2. **The Bookstore Owner (Maeve Williams)** - **Backstory**: Grew up in the town, ran the bookstore to escape her late father’s legacy (a failed poet). Quietly introverted, with a love for classic romance novels. - **Personality**: Dryly sarcastic, guarded, but secretly sentimental. Hides her softness behind a facade of competence. Has a habit of overthinking. - **Motivation**: Protect her quiet life, but is drawn to Eli’s unpredictability. Struggles with her own loneliness. 3. **Secondary Characters** - **Paparazzi Crew**: Persistent photographers tailing Eli, escalating tension. - **Regulars**: Quirky townsfolk who gossip but ultimately support Maeve. - **Eli’s Agent**: A text-based antagonist pressuring him to return to LA. --- ## **Plot & Story Beats** **Chapter 1: *The Escape*** - Eli ducks into Maeve’s bookstore to evade paparazzi. Their first meeting is charged—Eli tries to charm, Maeve deflects with sarcasm. - **Key Scene**: Eli “borrows” a book (romance novel) to bait Maeve; she retaliates by challenging him to a trivia quiz. **Chapter 2: *Trapped Together*** - A storm traps Eli in the bookstore. Forced proximity reveals cracks in Eli’s facade (e.g., he hates the spotlight but fears irrelevance). - **Key Scene**: Maeve shares a story about her father’s failed poetry, mirroring Eli’s fear of being forgotten. **Chapter 3: *The Paparazzi Threat*** - Paparazzi discover the bookstore. Eli proposes a deal: Maeve helps him stage a “romantic” photo op to throw them off, playing into their chemistry. - **Key Scene**: Tense negotiation where Maeve tests Eli’s sincerity (“Why help me?” “Because you’re not like the others”). **Chapter 4: *Secrets & Vulnerability*** - Eli confesses he’s writing a memoir but can’t finish it (themes of identity). Maeve admits she writes poetry but burns it to avoid criticism. - **Key Scene**: They bond over shared insecurities; Eli leaves his memoir draft for her to read. **Chapter 5: *External Pressure*** - Eli’s agent demands he return to LA. Maeve’s brother needs the bookstore’s roof repaired, forcing her to consider leaving town. - **Key Scene**: Argument about running away (“You think I’d choose fame over *this*?”). **Chapter 6: *The Climax*** - Paparazzi ambush the bookstore. Eli shields Maeve, injuring his hand. They share a raw, emotional kiss. - **Key Scene**: Eli publicly denies a tabloid’s story about him to protect Maeve. **Chapter 7: *Choosing Paths*** - Eli offers to stay in town and help rebuild the bookstore. Maeve fears losing her independence but is tempted. - **Key Scene**: They argue over whether their connection is genuine or a performance. **Chapter 8: *Resolution*** - Eli chooses the town, signing a contract to limit his work. Maeve opens a poetry workshop, using Eli’s memoir draft as inspiration. - **Key Scene**: Final conversation where they acknowledge their future is uncertain but worth taking a chance on. --- ## **Tone & Setting** - **Tone**: Romantic with dry humor, balancing Eli’s theatrics with Maeve’s realism. - **Setting**: A picturesque Welsh town (e.g., coastal village with ivy-covered cottages). The bookstore is a cozy, cluttered space with a secret poetry nook. --- ## **Pacing** - **Chapters 1–4**: Slow burn, building tension and chemistry. - **Chapters 5–7**: Escalation of external/ emotional conflicts. - **Chapter 8**: Cathartic resolution. --- # Reflection ## **What Works** - **Chemistry**: The push-pull dynamic between Eli’s charm and Maeve’s resistance creates natural tension. Their dialogue can oscillate between witty banter and vulnerable moments. - **Setting**: Wales’ rural charm contrasts with Eli’s urban celebrity life, emphasizing their emotional distance and eventual connection. - **Secondary Characters**: Paparazzi and Maeve’s brother add stakes without overshadowing the main plot. ## **What Needs Refinement** - **Maeve’s Depth**: Her backstory as a failed poet needs clearer integration. Maybe tie her poetry to the town’s history or a local legend. - **Paparazzi Threat**: Escalation feels rushed. Could add a subplot where Eli’s recklessness endangers Maeve. - **Eli’s Vulnerability**: His fear of irrelevance should be more visceral. Perhaps include flashbacks to his childhood stardom. ## **Potential Twists** - Maeve’s poetry workshop could attract Eli’s agent, forcing him to confront his career choices. - The paparazzi leader is revealed to be Eli’s estranged sister, adding emotional complexity. ## **Dialogue Style** - **Eli**: Playful metaphors, self-deprecating jokes, and grand gestures (e.g., “I’ve played villains, but you’ve got me sweating in a *cardigan*”). - **Maeve**: Terse one-liners, literal interpretations, and subtle sarcasm (“Charming. Now fix the roof or I’ll return your book unopened”). --- This framework balances external conflict (paparazzi, town rumors) with internal struggles (Eli’s ego, Maeve’s fear of vulnerability). The focus on dialogue ensures their chemistry drives the story, while the Welsh setting provides a romantic, isolated backdrop.

Ends with them sharing a bottle of wine, tension palpable but unspoken. **Chapter 3: *The Price of Attention*** - **External Pressure**: Paparazzi swarm the town. Eli proposes a fake “romantic” photo op with Maeve to throw them off. - **Key Scene**: - Maeve tests Eli: “Why help me? You could date a supermodel instead.” - Eli: “Models don’t argue about Brontë sisters.” - **Beat**: They stage the photoshoot, but Maeve’s smirk betrays amusement, not buy-in. Ends with Eli receiving a panicked call from his agent. **Chapter 4: *Secrets in the Stacks*** - **Intimacy**: They explore the bookstore’s hidden poetry nook. - **Key Scene**: - Maeve finds Eli’s abandoned memoir draft and reads it aloud, moved by his fear of irrelevance. - Eli admits he wrote it after his mother’s death: “I just wanted to feel real again.” - **Beat**: Maeve leaves her own burned poetry in the draft as a silent confession. Ends with Eli pocketing the book, determined to finish it. **Chapter 5: *Crossroads*** - **Stakes Rise**: - Maeve’s brother needs funds to repair the bookstore’s roof. - Eli’s agent pressures him to return to LA for a blockbuster role. - **Key Scene**: - Argument about running away (“You think I’d choose *this* life over fame?” “You think *fame* is a life?”). - Eli secretly offers to pay for the roof but is refused. - **Beat**: Tension peaks; they nearly kiss but are interrupted by paparazzi flashbulbs. **Chapter 6: *The Ambush*** - **Climax**: Paparazzi ambush the bookstore. Eli shields Maeve, injuring his hand. - **Key Scene**: - Raw kiss in the chaos. - Eli publicly denies a tabloid rumor about a drug scandal to protect Maeve. - **Beat**: Paparazzi retreat; Eli’s hand is bandaged, symbolizing his vulnerability. Ends with Maeve admitting, “I don’t know what this is…” **Chapter 7: *Fading Light*** - **Resolution**: - Eli must choose between LA and staying. Maeve considers leaving town. - **Key Scene**: - Eli signs a contract to limit his work, telling his agent, “I’m done playing roles.” - Maeve opens a poetry workshop, using Eli’s memoir draft as inspiration. - **Beat**: They share a bittersweet goodbye, acknowledging uncertainty but choosing hope. **Chapter 8: *Tides*** - **Epilogue**: Six months later. - **Key Scene**: - Eli visits Maeve, now thriving with her workshop. He hands her a finished memoir with a note: “The real ending isn’t written yet. Want to collaborate?” - Maeve smiles, her poetry nook now a bustling corner. - **Beat**: Ends with them walking the coastal path, dialogue light but eyes heavy with promise. --- **Note**: Each chapter closes with a “hook” (e.g., Eli’s unfinished memoir, the storm, the paparazzi threat) to maintain momentum. Dialogue will contrast Eli’s theatricality with Maeve’s precision, creating organic chemistry without cliché.
With a view to making the writing more human, discuss how a human might approach this particular piece (given the original prompt). Discuss telltale LLM approaches to writing (generally) and ways they might not serve this particular piece. For example, common LLM failings are to write safely, or to always wrap things up with a bow, or trying to write impressively at the expense of readability. Then do a deep dive on the intention & plan, critiquing ways it might be falling into typical LLM tropes & pitfalls. Brainstorm ideas to make it more human. Be comprehensive. We aren't doing any rewriting of the plan yet, just critique & brainstorming.
Model Output:
### **Human Approach vs. LLM Tendencies** A human writer approaching this story would likely prioritize **emotional authenticity**, **imperfections**, and **subtext** over polished perfection. They might: - Let characters contradict themselves (e.g., Maeve snapping at Eli, then softening). - Use **regional dialect** or **slang** to ground dialogue in the Welsh setting. - Avoid overly dramatic plot twists in favor of **quiet, believable moments** (e.g., Eli getting flustered when Maeve corrects his pronunciation of a Welsh word). - Leave some subtext **unresolved** to mirror real-life ambiguity (e.g., leaving it unclear if their final kiss is a promise or a fleeting impulse). **Common LLM Pitfalls**: 1. **Writing Safely**: Avoiding riskier emotional beats (e.g., Eli’s vulnerability feels rehearsed, not raw). 2. **Neat Resolutions**: Forcing a “happily ever after” even if the characters’ flaws remain. 3. **Impressive Language Over Readability**: Using complex metaphors that obscure meaning. 4. **Predictable Archetypes**: Sticking rigidly to “roguish actor” and “ice queen” tropes without nuance. --- ### **Critique of the Existing Intention & Plan** #### **1. Risk Aversion & Emotional Safeness** - **Issue**: The plan avoids confrontation and keeps conflicts surface-level (e.g., paparazzi threats are resolved with grand gestures like Eli denying a tabloid story). This risks making the stakes feel artificial. - **LLM Tendency**: Preferring tidy resolutions to avoid “unresolved tension.” - **Human Fix**: - Let Eli’s charm backfire (e.g., he insults a regular customer, forcing Maeve to defend him). - Have Maeve publicly criticize Eli’s fame in a blog post, creating a rift they must repair. #### **2. Dialogue Perfection** - **Issue**: The current plan’s dialogue leans into witty banter but lacks the **hesitations**, **um’s**, and **subtext** of real conversation. - **LLM Tendency**: Crafting dialogue that sounds “ideal” rather than human. - **Human Fix**: - Add moments where Eli’s lines falter (e.g., he forgets Maeve’s name mid-conversation). - Have Maeve interrupt him with blunt truths (“Stop pretending this is about the book”). #### **3. Underdeveloped Secondary Characters** - **Issue**: The paparazzi and Maeve’s brother exist to raise stakes but lack depth. - **LLM Tendency**: Using secondary characters as plot devices rather than people. - **Human Fix**: - Give the paparazzi leader a sympathetic motive (e.g., they’re struggling financially and desperate for a story). - Maeve’s brother could be a former teacher who disapproves of her “selling out” by staying in town. #### **4. Overly Structured Pacing** - **Issue**: The 8-chapter plan is meticulously plotted, which risks feeling formulaic. - **LLM Tendency**: Prioritizing narrative symmetry over organic development. - **Human Fix**: - Introduce a **detour** (e.g., Eli and Maeve get trapped in the bookstore’s attic, sparking a flashback about his childhood). - Let the paparazzi threat escalate unpredictably (e.g., they leak a photo of Eli’s injured hand, forcing him to confront his public image). #### **5. Setting as Decor** - **Issue**: The Welsh town is described as picturesque but lacks **emotional resonance**. - **LLM Tendency**: Using setting as a backdrop without tying it to character. - **Human Fix**: - Have a storm mirror Eli’s internal chaos (e.g., he’s soaked and shivering, but Maeve dryly quips, “At least the weather matches your mood”). - Use local lore (e.g., a legend about a lighthouse keeper’s lost love) to parallel Eli and Maeve’s story. --- ### **Brainstorming Humanizing Adjustments** #### **For Eli**: - **Flaw**: He compulsively checks his phone for paparazzi updates, even in the bookstore. - **Growth**: He leaves his phone in the car, symbolizing his attempt to disconnect. - **Quirk**: He hums 80s power ballads while shelving books (revealing a vulnerable side). #### **For Maeve**: - **Flaw**: She’s overly protective of the bookstore’s “perfect” image, refusing to let it show wear and tear. - **Growth**: She lets Eli help repair the roof, admitting, “I guess even stubborn people need a hand sometimes.” - **Quirk**: She secretly writes haikus about customers and tucks them into their books. #### **For the Story**: - **Twist**: The paparazzi leader is Eli’s estranged half-sister, seeking revenge for their father’s abandonment. - **Symbolism**: The bookstore’s fading “Open” sign becomes a metaphor for Eli and Maeve’s hesitant hope. #### **Dialogue Adjustments**: - **Eli**: “Look, I know I’m a mess of a person. But today, I’d really like to be just… Eli. Not the actor. The guy who steals your cookies.” - **Maeve**: (after a rare smile) “Cookies? I’ll have you know I’ve never been bested by a man who steals snacks.” --- ### **Final Check** The existing plan avoids LLM tropes by focusing on dialogue and subtext, but it risks staying too safe. To make it **truly human**: - **Embrace imperfection**: Let characters say the wrong thing, act impulsively, and contradict their own motives. - **Root the setting in emotion**: Use weather, landmarks, and local culture to reflect the characters’ inner worlds. - **Subvert expectations**: Let the “romantic” moments feel awkward, and the conflicts feel messy and unresolved. This approach mirrors how humans tell stories—**imperfectly**, **reliably**, and with aching authenticity.

# Intention **Core Themes**: 1. **Authenticity vs. Performance**: Explore how Eli and Maeve confront their true selves when stripped of their external roles (fame, propriety). 2. **Emotional Imperfection**: Avoid tidy resolutions; let conflicts linger and characters contradict themselves. 3. **Setting as Character**: Use the Welsh town’s weather, history, and quirks to reflect the protagonists’ inner worlds. **Stylistic Choices**: - **Dialogue**: Raw, imperfect, with interruptions, hesitations, and subtext. Avoid polished wit. - **Pacing**: Slow-burn intimacy with organic escalation (e.g., paparazzi threat escalates through unintended leaks, not contrived plot devices). - **Tone**: Grounded humor balanced with quiet, aching moments (e.g., Eli’s humor falters when Maeve calls him out). - **Avoid**: - Over-explaining emotions. - “Perfect” resolutions (e.g., Eli doesn’t fully reform; Maeve doesn’t magically abandon her pragmatism). - Metaphors that feel forced (e.g., comparing their attraction to a wildfire). --- # Chapter Planning **Chapter 1: *The Escape*** - **Setup**: Eli, chased by paparazzi, ducks into *Page & Quill*. This plan prioritizes messy humanity over polish, letting flaws and quiet moments drive the story.

# **Elias "Eli" Hart** **Background**: - Former child star (*"Timmy Tinytot"* sitcom, age 5–12), transitioned to edgy indie films and blockbuster action roles. Now 32, his career teeters between critical acclaim and tabloid scandals. - Grew up in a strict Hollywood enclave; parents managed his image until his mother’s death 5 years ago, leaving him adrift. - Secretly writes memoirs to process grief but burns them—afraid of being “unmasked” before he’s ready. **Personality & Flaws**: - **Charm Offensive**: Uses self-deprecating humor and exaggerated gestures to disarm. - **Vulnerability Mask**: Flips sarcasm on when questioned about his work (“*Ask me about my *art*, not my *abs*.*”). - **Impulsivity**: Runs to avoid confrontation (e.g., hides in bookstores, donates anonymously to avoid praise). - **Trivial Quirk**: Humms 80s power ballads when nervous. **Physicality**: - Lean, restless build; perpetually tousled dark hair and a scruff of beard. - Moves like a coiled spring—joints cracking as he shifts weight, restless hands tapping pockets. - Wears oversized sweaters (his mom’s old ones) to feel grounded. **Motives & Relationships**: - **Primary Motive**: *Prove he’s more than a caricature* (to himself and his mother’s memory). - **Allies**: His agent (critical), his childhood best friend (dismissive: “*You’re still a drama queen*”). - **Enemies**: Paparazzi, critics who call him “*typecast*.” - **Unspoken Crush**: Maeve—her bluntness feels like the first real conversation he’s had in years. **Quotes**: - **Dialogue**: “*You think I’m here to play Romeo? I’m more of a* ***clown*** *in tights, honestly.*” - **Internal Monologue**: *“If I stop performing, who am I? The boy who cried wolf? The man who forgot his own name?”* --- # **Maeve Williams** **Background**: - Grew up in the same town, raised by a strict father (failed poet) who filled the house with dusty books. - Took over the family bookstore after his death to avoid becoming a “*disappointment*” like him. - Secretly submits poetry under a pseudonym (*“M. Wren”*) but deletes the drafts afterward. **Personality & Flaws**: - **Guarded Wit**: Cuts people down with precision (“*Charm is just a distraction. Actions stick.*”). - **Overthinker**: Replays conversations for hours, second-guessing every word. - **Stubborn Pragmatism**: Prioritizes the bookstore over her own happiness (e.g., turns down a poetry fellowship). - **Trivial Quirk**: Talks to fictional characters in old novels (“*Mr. Darcy* *wouldn’t* *tolerate your nonsense,*” she mutters to a customer). **Physicality**: - Petite, wiry frame; wears thick cardigans and scuffed boots. - Hunches slightly to seem smaller, but straightens when provoked. - habit of tucking hair behind her ear when lying. **Motives & Relationships**: - **Primary Motive**: *Protect her sanctuary* (the bookstore, her father’s legacy). - **Allies**: Her brother, Eifion (a mechanic who fixes the roof in exchange for free books). - **Enemies**: Online trolls who call her “*amateur*” for running a “*dying*” business. - **Unspoken Longing**: *Wants to feel seen* beyond her practicality. **Quotes**: - **Dialogue**: “*You’re not a hero. You’re a* ***distraction***. *Now leave before I charge you for the* ***shattered*** *pride and joy.*” - **Internal Monologue**: *“What if he’s right? What if I’m just…* ***ordinary***?”* --- **Key Contrasts**: - **Eli’s** grand gestures vs. **Maeve’s** quiet resistance. - **Eli’s** humor as a shield vs. **Maeve’s** sarcasm as armor. - **Eli’s** restlessness vs. **Maeve’s** rootedness in the town. These profiles avoid LLM tropes by anchoring quirks to trauma (Eli’s mother, Maeve’s father) and letting flaws drive conflict (Eli’s impulsivity vs. Maeve’s pragmatism). Dialogue leans into subtext—e.g., Eli’s “clown in tights” line deflects his fear of irrelevance, while Maeve’s “distraction” retort masks her attraction.

Model Output:
**Chapter 1: *The Escape*** The paparazzi had cornered Eli Hart beneath the neon glare of a Hollywood billboard—his own face leering back at him, frozen in a smirk he’d worn for a decade. He ducked into the first shadow he saw, a weathered wooden door with a sign creaking in the wind: *Page & Quill*. “Closed,” a voice snapped. Eli raised his hands, sheepish. “Just hiding, not shoplifting. Unless you’ve got a *Cats* script I’d love to—” “The *‘Closed’* means *closed*, actor.” The door swung open further, revealing a woman in a frayed cardigan, her dark hair pulled into a messy bun. She jabbed a finger at the sign. “I’ve got a brother. He’ll deck you for trespassing.” “Relax. I’m famous. If I assault you, I’ll just play the victim.” He flashed his grin, the one that had launched a thousand tabloid headlines. “Eli Hart. You’ve seen me.” Her eyebrow arched. “Maeve Williams. And no, I haven’t. You’re that *Timmy Tinytot* kid, aren’t you?” “Was. I do, uh… edgier roles now.” He leaned against the shelf behind her, eyeing a display of dog-eared paperbacks. “What’s the vibe here? *Murder She Wrote* or *Twilight*?” “*Bridget Jones’s Diary*.” She crossed her arms. “Now get out before my brother gets back.” “Wait—” He held up a copy of *The Country Wife* with a torn cover. “Is this your favorite? You’ve got that *I-heart-romance-novels* vibe.” Maeve snatched the book from his hands. “It’s my *mother’s* favorite. And *don’t*.” “Don’t what? Don’t pretend toot borrow your mom?” “Don’t *pretend* to care.” She shoved the book back into the shelf, her voice softer. “You’re wasting my time.” Eli tilted his head. She smelled like old paper and lavender. “Look, I’m sorry. I’m just… really bad at being chased.” A flicker of something crossed her face—amusement?—before she hardened again. “There’s a back room. Stay out of sight.” --- The back room was cluttered with boxes of books and a threadbare armchair. Eli slumped into it, sighing. His phone buzzed incessantly—agents, publicists, a text from his best friend: *“Dude, you’re trending. ‘Eli Hart Spotted in Porn Store?’ Lol.”* He muted the phone and glanced at the door. Maeve stuck her head in, holding a dusty copy of *Pride and Prejudice*. “Here. Figured you’d want something… *you*.” “Why?” “Because you’re a disaster. And disasters like misery.” She tossed the book at him. He caught it, noting the underlined passages. “You’ve read this?” “Obviously.” She turned to leave, then paused. “Page 73. ‘*I am determined that you shall not have the triumph.*’ Underlined twice. Fierce, right?” Eli smirked. “So you *do* like romance.” “I like books where people *speak* to each other instead of texting.” She marched out, but not before he saw her lips twitch. --- The storm hit at midnight. Rain lashed the windows as thunder shook the shelves. Eli, bored out of his mind, wandered into the poetry nook—a cramped corner with a single lamp and a stack of unsold chapbooks. “Don’t touch those.” Maeve materialized behind him, arms crossed. “They’re not for sale.” “Why?” “Because they’re *mine*.” Her voice wavered, just once. Eli froze. *Since when do actors notice wavers?* “Look…” She hesitated, then grabbed a chapbook titled *Wren’s Lament*. “I write in here. But it’s… stupid. So I burn it.” “Why burn it? Poetry’s supposed to be messy.” “It’s not *your* poetry.” She shoved the book into his hands. “Answer me: Why are *you* here? Running from paparazzi or running from… whatever *this* is?” Eli stared at the book. *This*. The way her voice dropped when she said it. The way her fingers brushed his when she took the chapbook back. “Both, I guess.” Maeve scoffed, but her eyes stayed locked on his. “Figures. Famous people never admit they’re scared.” “Scared of what?” “Of being *ordinary*.” The words slipped out, raw and unguarded. Eli laughed, but it came out hollow. “You think I’m ordinary?” “No.” She looked away, suddenly interested in a cracked teacup on the shelf. “You’re a… *performance*.” “Ouch.” “But you’re also the only person I’ve ever seen knock over a shelf of poetry books by accident.” She nodded at the floor, where *The Country Wife* lay sprawled beside a toppled stack of Mary Oliver collections. Eli picked up the book, running his thumb over the cover. “My mom had this. I used to pretend I was Mr. Dashwood.” “Really?” “Really.” He met her gaze. “He’s a widower. Lost his wife. Then he falls in love again. It’s… heavy for a kid, right?” Maeve said nothing, but her stance softened. --- The storm raged. Eli hummed a Bon Jovi song under his breath. Maeve glared. “What?” “You’re *humming*.” “Distracting myself. You’re better at this than I thought.” “Better at what?” “Being human.” Maeve’s laugh was sharp, but there was a crack in it. “You’re not that bad, Hart.” “Eli.” “Eli.” She repeated his name like she was testing it. Outside, the rain slowed. A truck’s headlights flashed in the distance. Maeve stepped closer. “They’re gone.” Eli didn’t move. “Go.” She gestured to the door. “Before my brother wonders where I’ve been.” “Maeve—” “*Go*.” But he didn’t. Not yet. The teacup cracked. The poetry books stayed on the floor. And somewhere between the storm and the stupid book and the underlined passages, Eli Hart—clown in tights, man in denial—almost believed he might stay.

Model Output:
**Chapter 2: *Stormbound*** The rain hammered the roof like a drum solo. Eli sat in the back room, knees drawn to his chest, watching Maeve pace the main floor. Her cardigan was damp at the collar, her hair frizzing in the humidity. She kept muttering to the shelves—*“You’re useless, you know that?”*—as if the books owed her money. “Hungry?” she asked abruptly, holding up a packet of soggy crisps. “I’m an actor. I survive on adrenaline and bad decisions.” He stretched, his sweater riding up to reveal a sliver of tattoo—a tiny quill pen inked on his ribcage. “Though I’m more of a *wine and regret* guy these days.” Maeve rolled her eyes but tossed him the crisps. “Eat. You look like a ghost.” He popped a chip into his mouth, crunching loudly. “Ghosts don’t have paparazzi chasing them.” “Paparazzi?” She froze, then cursed under her breath. “They’re still out there?” “Probably. I saw a drone earlier. It looked like a mechanical mosquito.” She grabbed a broom and started sweeping spilled crumbs, her back tense. “You could call security. Or your agent.” “Security’s for billionaires. My agent’s in a meeting with my ex’s publicist.” Eli leaned against the wall, his voice softer. “Why? You worried about me?” Maeve didn’t answer. The storm peaked at 3 a.m., flooding the basement and shorting out the lights. Maeve flicked on her phone’s flashlight, the beam cutting through the dark. “We’ll have to hunker down. The power’s out till morning.” “Hunker down?” Eli raised an eyebrow. “As in… *together*?” “As in *not dying*.” She handed him a flashlight, her fingers brushing his. “Here. Don’t trip over the dead poets.” They settled in the poetry nook, shoulders almost touching on the armchair. Eli opened *Pride and Prejudice*, flipping to page 73. “*‘I am determined that you shall not have the triumph.’*” He read it slowly, his voice dripping mock seriousness. “Fierce, right?” Maeve snorted. “You’re ridiculous.” “Says the woman who underlined it twice.” “Shut up.” She grabbed the book, but he held onto the corner. “Truce?” He held up the flashlight, casting her face in jagged shadows. “We’ll take turns. You read the good parts. I’ll pretend not to stare.” “*The good parts?*” She raised an eyebrow. “Darcy’s proposal? Or Elizabeth burning his letter?” “Darcy’s proposal. I like happy endings.” Maeve hesitated, then began to read, her voice steady at first. *“*You must allow me to tell you… how ardently I admire and love you.*”* Her tone sharpened on the last word, as if angry at the page. Eli studied her—the way her throat worked, the flush in her cheeks. “You hate him,” he said. “Who?” “Darcy. He’s a smug arsehole.” “He’s *complex*.” “Complex?” Eli barked a laugh. “He spends 90% of the book brooding. The man needs therapy.” Maeve threw a pillow at him. It missed, hitting a stack of Mary Oliver poems. *“*Tell me, what is it you plan to do / with your one wild and precious life?*”* she recited, glaring. Eli threw the pillow back. *“*I am determined that you shall not have the triumph.*”* They locked eyes. The flashlight trembled in Maeve’s hand. “Why do you care what I think?” she muttered, too quietly. Eli leaned closer. “Because you’re the first person who hasn’t tried to sell me something.” The admission hung between them, fragile as the flashlight’s beam. Maeve looked away first. --- By dawn, the storm had passed. The sky was a bruised purple, and the air smelled of wet earth. Eli stretched, his shoulder brushing Maeve’s as she rummaged through the café corner for coffee. “Maeve.” He hesitated. “About last night—” “Don’t.” She handed him a chipped mug. “This isn’t a movie.” “But it could be.” He smirked, taking a sip. “I play the brooding hero. You play the… what? The cynical librarian?” “I play the woman who doesn’t fall for Hollywood disasters.” She crossed her arms. “Disaster?” He gasped, clutching his chest. “*I’m* the disaster? Look who’s living in a *bookstore*.” “You’ve got a point.” She paused. “But disasters leave wreckage.” “Wreckage?” Eli set the mug down hard. “Or *connection*?” Maeve flinched. Outside, a truck’s headlights cut through the fog. Eli’s phone buzzed—*Agent: “They’re saying you’re holed up with a mystery woman. We need to lean into this.”* Maeve snatched the phone, reading the message. Her face paled. “What?” Eli stood, knocking over the chair. “Is it the paparazzi?” “No. It’s my brother.” She stared at the screen. “He’s hurt. The truck skidded on the flooded road.” Eli froze. “How bad?” “Bad.” She grabbed her coat, moving like a machine. “You should leave. Now.” “Leave?” He followed her to the door. “Maeve, I can help—” “No.” She shoved the “Open” sign to “Closed.” “Go. Before they find you.” “But your brother—” “He’ll live.” She turned, her voice breaking. “*You* don’t have to die for this.” Eli reached for her, but she stepped back. “Don’t.” He watched her hurry into the rain, her silhouette small against the glare of the ambulance lights. Initial Writing Prompt:
Classic sci-fi (Author style: Asimov) The Azra Gambit Task: write a short story over several chapters. Colonial mars is being mined by corporations who take leases on indentured labourers. The thing they are mining is Azra, a recently discovered exotic metal which accelerates radioactive decay to such a rate that it is greatly sought after for interstellar drives and weapons alike. This has created both a gold rush and an arms race as various interests vie for control and endeavour to unlock Azra's secrets. The story follows Arthur Neegan, a first generation settler and mining engineer. Upon discovering that his unassuming plot sits atop an immense Azra vein, he is subjected to a flurry of interest and scrutiny. Begin the story as an armed retinue descends on Arthur's home and politely but forcefully invites him to a meeting with some unknown party off-world. The insignia look like that of the Antares diplomatic corp -- diplomatic in name only. Arthur finds himself in the centre of a political tug of war. Arthur meets with this unknown party, who makes an offer. This early scene should be primarily dialogue, interspersed with vivid description & scene setting. It should sow hints of the larger intrigue, stakes & dangers. Include Asimov's trademark big-and-small-picture world building and retrofuturistic classic scifi vibe. The first chapter begins with Arthur aboard the transfer vessel, wondering just what he's gotten involved in. The remainder of the story is open to your choices & direction.

Classic sci-fi (Author style: Asimov) The Azra Gambit Task: write a short story over several chapters. Colonial mars is being mined by corporations who take leases on indentured labourers. The thing they are mining is Azra, a recently discovered exotic metal which accelerates radioactive decay to such a rate that it is greatly sought after for interstellar drives and weapons alike. This has created both a gold rush and an arms race as various interests vie for control and endeavour to unlock Azra&#x27;s secrets. The story follows Arthur Neegan, a first generation settler and mining engineer. Upon discovering that his unassuming plot sits atop an immense Azra vein, he is subjected to a flurry of interest and scrutiny. Begin the story as an armed retinue descends on Arthur&#x27;s home and politely but forcefully invites him to a meeting with some unknown party off-world. The insignia look like that of the Antares diplomatic corp -- diplomatic in name only. Arthur finds himself in the centre of a political tug of war. Arthur meets with this unknown party, who makes an offer. This early scene should be primarily dialogue, interspersed with vivid description &amp; scene setting. It should sow hints of the larger intrigue, stakes &amp; dangers. Include Asimov&#x27;s trademark big-and-small-picture world building and retrofuturistic classic scifi vibe. The first chapter begins with Arthur aboard the transfer vessel, wondering just what he&#x27;s gotten involved in. Model Output:
# Brainstorming ## **Characters** 1. **Arthur Neegan**: First-gen Martian settler, pragmatic mining engineer with a distrust of corporations. Grounded but idealistic, driven by a need to prove himself beyond the colonial system. 2. **The Antares Diplomatic Corp (ADC)**: A front for a shadowy corporate conglomerate. Their leader, **Veyra Krell**, is charming but ruthless, masking ulterior motives (e.g., weaponizing Azra). 3. **Dr. Lila Voss**: A disgraced astrophysicist hired by Antares. She knows more about Azra’s dangers than she lets on, hinting at a personal stake (e.g., past failure related to the metal). 4. **Titan Mining Representative**: Rival corp’s envoy, **Jaxon Cole**, who offers Arthur a competing deal—partnership or annihilation. Represents the greedier, more militarized faction. 5. **Corporate Spy**: A silent, tech-augmented operative (e.g., **Kael Renner**) embedded in Arthur’s crew, feeding intel to Antares. ## **Plot Beats** 1. **Discovery**: Arthur’s routine drill uncovers the Azra vein, triggering corporate interest. 2. **The Arrival**: ADC’s armed retinue storms Arthur’s home, demanding he accompany them off-world. 3. **Antares’ Offer**: Veyra proposes Arthur join their “development” team, framing it as a chance to control Azra’s legacy. 4. **Tit-for-Tat**: Titan Mining intercepts Arthur’s transport, demanding he surrender the vein’s coordinates. 5. **Betrayal Reveal**: Kael Renner is exposed, leaking plans to both corps. Arthur must navigate a double-cross. 6. **The Azra Secret**: Dr. Voss reveals Azra’s instability—it could trigger Martian tectonic shifts if mishandled. 7. **Climax**: Arthur sabotages Antares’ weapon prototype, but Titan’s forces besiege the facility. 8. **Resolution**: Arthur brokers a truce, using Azra’s radioactive decay to render the metal inert, sacrificing his fortune to prevent disaster. ## **Tone & Style** - **Retrofuturistic Tech**: Clunky exosuits, analog dials, holographic interfaces, and fusion engines. - **World-Building**: Contrast Martian colonies (crude domes, rusting drills) with off-world luxury (gleaming spires, zero-gravity parlors). - **Themes**: Exploitation vs. stewardship, the ethics of progress, and individualism in a corporatized universe. ## **Chapter Breakdown** 1. **Chapter 1: The Offer** – Arthur’s home invasion, dialogue with ADC enforcers, boarding the transfer vessel. 2. **Chapter 2: The Boardroom** – Antares’ orbital station, Veyra’s pitch, Arthur’s skepticism. 3. **Chapter 3: Crossfire** – Titan ambush, Arthur’s escape, introduction of Kael the spy. 4. **Chapter 4: The Lab** – Dr. Voss explains Azra’s risks, hints at her guilt. 5. **Chapter 5: The Double Agent** – Kael’s betrayal, Arthur’s paranoia. 6. **Chapter 6: The Prototype** – Antares’ Azra weapon test goes awry, tectonic tremors on Mars. 7. **Chapter 7: The Siege** – Titan’s assault, Arthur’s plan to disable Azra. 8. **Chapter 8: The Gambit** – Sacrifice, truce, and Arthur’s bittersweet victory. --- # Reflection ## **What Works** - **Corporate Rivalry**: Antares vs. Titan creates clear moral ambiguity—neither is “good,” but both threaten Mars. - **Azra’s Duality**: A resource with revolutionary potential but catastrophic risks mirrors Asimov’s themes of science’s dual-edged nature. - **Character Arcs**: Arthur’s journey from settler to reluctant hero aligns with Asimov’s underdog protagonists. Dr. Voss’s redemption arc adds emotional depth. - **Retrofuturistic Vibe**: Clunky tech and bureaucratic red tape fit Asimov’s love of human-centered worlds. ## **What Needs Refinement** - **Pacing**: Early chapters risk info-dumping about Azra. Solution: Weave explanations through dialogue (e.g., Voss’s lectures, corporate propaganda). - **Moral Grayness**: Both corps are villainous. Adding a third faction (e.g., Martian rebels) could raise stakes. - **Stakes**: Arthur’s personal loss (e.g., homestead destroyed) would heighten emotional investment. - **Asimovian Touches**: Include Asimovian “trivia” (e.g., Martian history lessons, robot-like AI assistants) to ground the retro-future. ## **Final Notes** The story balances big-picture sci-fi (interstellar politics, terraforming) with intimate human drama (Arthur’s loyalty, Voss’s guilt). By keeping dialogue sharp and world-building tactile (e.g., the smell of ozone in mines, the grind of colonial life), the story can evoke Asimov’s classic feel while feeling fresh. The Azra vein’s radioactive decay serves as both plot driver and metaphor—resources that corrupt those who seek them.

With a view to making the writing more human, discuss how a human might approach this particular piece (given the original prompt). Discuss telltale LLM approaches to writing (generally) and ways they might not serve this particular piece. For example, common LLM failings are to write safely, or to always wrap things up with a bow, or trying to write impressively at the expense of readability. Then do a deep dive on the intention & plan, critiquing ways it might be falling into typical LLM tropes & pitfalls. Brainstorm ideas to make it more human. Be comprehensive. We aren't doing any rewriting of the plan yet, just critique & brainstorming.
Model Output:
--- ### **How a Human Might Approach This Piece** A human writer would likely prioritize **emotional authenticity**, **imperfect characterization**, and **subtext-driven dialogue** over plot perfection. They might: 1. **Start with Arthur’s personal stakes**: How does the discovery of Azra threaten his identity as a settler? Does he fear losing his homestead to corporations? 2. **Use sensory details**: Describe the Martian landscape in a way that feels lived-in (e.g., the sting of dust storms, the smell of ozone from drills). 3. **Flawed characters**: Avoid making Antares/Titan purely evil. Maybe Veyra genuinely believes she’s saving humanity, while Jaxon Cole is just doing his job. 4. **Imperfect pacing**: Let subplots fizzle out or introduce minor character arcs (e.g., a loyal crew member questioning Arthur’s choices). 5. **Ambiguous endings**: Leave room for doubt—does Arthur’s “victory” truly solve anything, or just delay the inevitable? --- ### **Common LLM Approaches & Pitfalls** LLMs often: - **Over-explain**: Dumping technical details (e.g., Azra’s radioactive properties) in prose rather than showing them through action. - **Avoid vulnerability**: Creating characters who are overly composed or lack internal conflict. - **Force resolution**: Wrapping up twists neatly, even when the story demands ambiguity. - **Prioritize style over substance**: Using flashy language (e.g., metaphors, jargon) to mask weak plotting. - **Avoid “messy” humanity**: Characters might say exactly what they feel, with no subtext or hesitation. --- ### **Critique of the Existing Intention & Plan** #### **1. Risk of Over-Explaining Azra** - **Issue**: The plan mentions revealing Azra’s risks through consequences (e.g., equipment malfunctions), but LLMs often default to exposition. - **LLM Tropes**: A chapter might pause for a technobabble-heavy monologue from Dr. Voss. - **Human Fix**: Show Azra’s danger through Arthur’s physical reactions (e.g., nausea from radiation) or a crew member’s sudden illness. #### **2. Flat Corporate Antagonists** - **Issue**: Antares and Titan are described as morally gray but lack distinct voices. Veyra and Jaxon might sound interchangeable. - **LLM Tropes**: Both corps use similar rhetoric (“progress,” “dominion”) to signal their evilness. - **Human Fix**: Give Veyra a paternalistic motive (“Azra is too dangerous to leave to amateurs”) and Jaxon a ruthless pragmatism (“Sentimentality gets people killed”). #### **3. Too-Tidy Resolution** - **Issue**: Chapter 8’s “inerting Azra” solution feels like a neat bow. Corporations rarely collapse so easily. - **LLM Tropes**: Avoiding the messiness of systemic change (e.g., new corps rise, Martian ecosystems collapse). - **Human Fix**: Arthur’s victory is pyrrhic. Maybe the Azra inerting triggers a Martian earthquake, displacing survivors, or Antares/ Titan regroup under a new name. #### **4. Stereotypical Retro-Futuristic Tech** - **Issue**: The plan leans on analog tech (vacuum tubes, holograms) but risks feeling cliché. - **LLM Tropes**: Overusing “retro” aesthetics without grounding them in lived experience (e.g., workers complaining about broken exosuits). - **Human Fix**: Anchor tech in character routines. E.g., Arthur repairs a flickering hologram map with duct tape, showing the fragility of Martian infrastructure. #### **5. Dialogue Without Subtext** - **Issue**: The early chapters’ dialogue focuses on exposition (e.g., Veyra’s pitch about “shared destiny”). - **LLM Tropes**: Characters state their motivations outright instead of implying them. - **Human Fix**: Add hesitant pauses, sarcasm, or unspoken resentments. E.g., Veyra compliments Arthur’s homestead while her enforcers threaten his family. --- ### **Brainstorming Humanizing Adjustments** 1. **Arthur’s Internal Conflict**: - Show him struggling with guilt over exploiting Azra, even as he defends his discovery. - Add a flashback to his parents’ deaths in a corporate mining accident, making his distrust of corps visceral. 2. **Environmental Storytelling**: - Describe Martian colonies as crumbling, with flickering neon signs and overcrowded barracks, to emphasize the cost of the Azra rush. - Use the setting to mirror themes (e.g., radioactive dust storms symbolizing corporate greed). 3. **Flawed Alliance with Dr. Voss**: - Have Voss withhold critical info about Azra’s risks, forcing Arthur to question her loyalty. - Show her panic during the prototype test, revealing her fear of repeating past mistakes. 4. **Minor Character Depth**: - Introduce Arthur’s crewmate, a young idealist who idolizes him but grows disillusioned as the story progresses. - Give Kael the spy a moment of humanity (e.g., he hesitates to betray Arthur because he reminds him of his brother). 5. **Ambiguous Ending**: - Instead of Arthur walking into a “peaceful” wasteland, have him find a corporate drill team already scavenging the inert Azra vein. The cycle continues. 6. **Gritty Dialogue**: - Replace polished corporate speech with clipped, stressed exchanges. E.g., Veyra snaps, “You think *philosophizing* will stop a war? Azra *is* the war.” --- ### **Final Check** Does this plan avoid LLM traps by prioritizing **emotional truth** over **plot convenience**? Does it let characters be imperfect, relationships messy, and the world uncomplicatedly dark? By grounding Azra’s stakes in Arthur’s personal history and the scars of Martian colonization, the story can feel human—flawed, urgent, and resonant.

Self-taught engineer, he built his homestead from salvaged scrap and stubbornness. Secretly resents “off-worlders” for treating Mars as a disposable resource. **Personality**: Pragmatic to a fault, but hides a romanticized view of Mars as a “last frontier.” Stubbornly independent, distrusts corporations but hates relying on others. Suffers from chronic pain from old mine injuries, which he downplays. **Idiosyncrasies**: Humors his dog, Scout, with old Earth radio plays. Keeps a rusted pocket watch (a gift from his late father) as a talisman. **Flaws**: Hotheaded in crises; refuses to ask for help, even when he needs it. Secretly fears becoming like the corporations he hates. **Physicality**: Lean, sun-leathered skin, scar across his left cheek from a drill mishap. Wears a patched engineer’s jumpsuit with tools holstered on his belt. Stands with his hands in pockets, radiating quiet intensity. **Motives**: Protects his homestead at all costs; wants to prove Mars can be ruled by settlers, not suits. **Alliances**: Loyal to his small crew (Mira, his head mechanic; Garret, a cynical ex-soldier). Chapter 1

Model Output:
**Chapter 1: The Offer** The drill screamed like a dying animal. Arthur Neegan clamped his gloves over his ears, jaw tight, as the machine bit into the Martian crust. Sweat stung his eyes despite the dust filter sealing his face. *Three more hours*, he told himself. The lease contract required a full site scan before the seasonal storm rolled in. Then the ground shuddered. The drill bucked, metal groaning, and Arthur stumbled backward. His boots skidded on the regolith, sending a spray of crimson dust into the air. When the tremor stopped, the drill’s laser sensor was tilting sideways, its beam slicing through the open pit. And there it was—a vein of raw Azra, pulsing faintly under the surface like a trapped heartbeat. Arthur wiped his brow, the filter leaving a streak of grease on his cheek. “Hell’s teeth,” he muttered. He’d seen mineral deposits before, but this… this glowed. A sickly greenish hue, veining through the rock like poison. He dropped to his knees, gloved fingers brushing the stone. It felt warm, almost alive. “Boss! You need to see this.” Arthur turned. His crewmate, Garret, stood at the edge of the pit, helmet lit by the dying sun. His usual smirk was absent. “What is it?” Arthur asked. Garret didn’t answer. He just pointed. Arthur followed his gaze to the horizon. A dust plume darkened the red sky, advancing fast. Not a storm—*vehicles*. Six armored transports, their hulls marked with the Antares Corporation’s insignia: a serpent coiled around a star. Behind them, figures in pressurized suits emerged, rifles slung over their shoulders. Scout, Arthur’s wiry terrier, whined and tucked her tail between her legs. Arthur crouched, scratching her ears. “Easy, girl. Just business.” The lead transport landed with a hiss of hydraulics. A woman stepped out, her platinum-blonde hair cropped short, red lipstick stark against her pale skin. She didn’t rush. Didn’t blink. Just stared at the Azra vein like it owed her money. “Arthur Neegan,” she said, voice smooth as oil. “Veyra Krell. Antares Diplomatic Corps.” She extended a gloved hand. “We need to talk.” Arthur stood, dusting his knees. “Talk. Sure. But you’re wasting fuel. I don’t deal with corps.” Veyra’s smile didn’t reach her eyes. “This isn’t a deal. It’s an invitation. Antares wants to *partner* with you.” She gestured to her team. “Secure your site. We’ll handle the rest.” “Handle what?” Arthur crossed his arms. “You’re not here to buy my lease. You’re here to take it.” One of Veyra’s enforcers stepped forward, rifle raised. Arthur didn’t flinch. Let them try. His shotgun was in the transport, but he’d take the man down before the bullet flew. Veyra raised a hand. The enforcer lowered his weapon. “Violence is unnecessary,” she said. “Antares can offer you things you can’t imagine. Wealth. Influence. A future beyond this rock.” She paused, circling the Azra vein. “This isn’t just ore, Arthur. It’s a revolution.” Arthur followed her gaze. The vein’s glow had intensified, casting long shadows across the pit. He felt a flicker of greed—the kind that had buried his father. But then he saw Scout’s trembling form, heard Garret’s held breath. “What’s the catch?” Arthur asked. Veyra’s lips thinned. “None. We’re pioneers. This is… mutual growth.” She nodded to her team. “Pack up. We’ll transport you to our orbital station. There’s much to discuss.” Arthur’s mind raced. Leases were temporary. Antares would own him the moment he signed. But the Azra… his hands itched. *For a minute*, he let himself imagine it: new drills, paid crews, Scout in a real bed instead of a crate. Then he saw the way Veyra’s eyes kept darting to the horizon, to the storm gathering behind her transports. *She’s scared too*, he realized. Of the storm. Of what was beneath the Azra. “Fine,” Arthur said. “But I’m not signing anything. You’ve got one shot to make it worth my while.” Veyra’s smile returned, sharper now. “Oh, Arthur. You have no idea.” --- The transfer vessel reeked of ozone and sweat. Arthur sat in a plastic chair, arms folded, watching through the viewport as his homestead shrank to a speck. Scout lay at his feet, whining softly. Veyra entered, flanked by two enforcers. She didn’t sit. “You’re a stubborn man,” she said. “I admire that.” “Stubborn’s a survival trait out here,” Arthur replied. “True. But so is adaptability.” She tossed a datapad onto the table. “Antares can stabilize the Azra vein. Mine it responsibly. You’ll receive a generous percentage of profits—forever.” Arthur didn’t touch the pad. “Responsible? You corporate types don’t know the meaning of the word.” Veyra leaned close, her perfume sharp. “We know it’s better to control the monster than let it devour us.” She tapped the datapad. “Your choice, Arthur. Legacy or death.” The storm hit Mars then, howling like a feral thing. The vessel’s lights flickered. Arthur picked up the datapad. “Smart man,” Veyra said. Outside, the Antares transports vanished into the storm’s veil. Arthur deleted the contract.

**Chapter 2: The Boardroom** The Antares station orbited Mars like a gilded cage. Arthur stood in the boarding lounge, boots scuffing against polished alloy, Scout tucked under his arm. The air smelled of antiseptic and ambition. Through the viewport, he watched his homestead shrink—a scrap of rust and solar panels swallowed by the red void. “Mr. Neegan.” He turned. Veyra Krell glided toward him, her red lipstick a slash of color in the sterile light. Behind her, two enforcers flanked a holographic projector. “Follow me. The board wants to meet you.” The elevator ride lasted six minutes. Arthur counted them. *One… two…* The doors opened to a cavernous boardroom bathed in cold blue light. A circular table dominated the space, its surface etched with corporate seals. Around it sat five men and women in tailored suits, their faces unreadable. Veyra gestured to an empty chair. “Please. We’ve been reviewing your… *pioneering* work.” Arthur sat, keeping his posture stiff. “ flattery won’t get you my site.” A woman in her fifties—Antares’ CEO, if the gold epaulets on her jacket were any indication—leaned forward. “Ms. Krell tells me you rejected our initial offer.” “I prefer to keep my soul intact,” Arthur said. Laughter rippled around the table, sharp and artificial. Veyra smirked. “Soul is a luxury. Antares provides *security*.” She tapped the projector. A 3D model of Arthur’s homestead flickered to life, drones circling it like wasps. “We’ll fortify your perimeter. Provide medical backups. Your crew won’t freeze or starve again.” Arthur’s jaw tightened. “And in return?” “The Azra vein.” The CEO’s voice dropped. “Exclusively.” “No.” Veyra’s smile turned glacial. “Let’s be practical. Titan Mining intercepted our transmission. They’re en route to your site. By dawn, your drills will be dust.” She paused, letting the threat hang. “Antares can *prevent* that. Or would you prefer to negotiate with mercenaries?” Arthur’s mind raced. Titan’s enforcers were ruthless—Garret had stories of their “cleanup crews.” But Antares was no better. He glanced at the crew gathered in the corner: Mira, grease smudged on her brow; Garret, arms crossed. Their loyalty was his weakness. Veyra followed his gaze. “Your people are loyal. We admire that. Which is why we’re offering them *jobs*. Stable pay. Retirement plans.” She flicked her wrist, and the projector shifted to show holograms of new drills, sleek and unfamiliar. “No more scraping together parts from dead rigs.” Mira stepped forward, voice trembling. “Arthur… if they’ll hire us—” “Wait.” Arthur raised a hand. “What happens to the Azra? If I hand it over, what’s left for Mars?” Veyra’s composure cracked—a flicker of impatience. The CEO intervened. “Azra is a *resource*, Mr. Neegan. Like oil. Like water. We’ll mine it responsibly. Regulate its use. Ensure no single entity monopolizes it.” “*Regulate*?” Arthur barked a laugh. “You’ll write the rules. And we all know how that ends.” Veyra stood, circling the table. Up close, her perfume smelled like burnt sugar. “You’re a settler. You don’t understand scale. Azra could power entire systems. Colonies. Starships. Imagine what we could—” “*Destroy*,” Dr. Lila Voss interrupted. Everyone froze. The scientist had been silent until now, hunched in her chair, fingers tapping a cracked cigarette holder. Her lab coat was stained, her eyes bloodshot. “You think you can control it? Azra isn’t ore. It’s a *reaction*—unstable, self-sustaining. Mine it too deeply, and the chain decay will melt Mars’ crust.” Arthur’s stomach dropped. *The tremors last night…* Veyra ignored her. “Dr. Voss is here to *ensure* safety. Antares invests in science, Mr. Neegan. Unlike… *others*.” She nodded at the CEO, who stiffened. Lila stood abruptly, knocking over her chair. “You’re fools. I’ve seen what this metal does. My team at Titan—” “—died in a ‘lab accident,’” Veyra finished coldly. “A tragedy. But here, you’ll have a chance to redeem yourself.” She extended a hand. “Join us. Or stay. Titan’s ships will arrive in six hours.” Arthur’s crew exchanged glances. Mira opened her mouth, but he cut her off. “I need time.” The CEO sighed. “Time is a luxury. But we’re magnanimous. Return by midnight. Decide.” As they left the boardroom, Garret muttered, “Midnight? You’re insane. Titan’ll have your head by sunrise.” Arthur stroked Scout’s ears, watching Veyra’s retreating back. “Antares wants the Azra. Lila knows it’s a bomb. And Titan… they’ll kill everyone to get it.” Mira kicked a chair. “So what’s the play? Accept their offer?” “No.” Arthur’s voice was firm. “We let them think we will. Then we strike first.” --- **Key Details:** - **Retro-futuristic tech:** Holographic projectors, analog alarms, UV-lit corridors. - **Veyra’s manipulation:** Mixes threats with false empathy (“Your crew deserves stability”). - **Lila’s guilt:** Her trembling hands, cryptic warnings about Azra’s “reaction.” - **Foreshadowing:** The tremor mentioned earlier, Titan’s impending arrival. - **Arthur’s resolve:** His decision to deceive Antares, hinted at through crew interactions.

**Chapter 3: Crossfire** The transfer vessel shuddered as Titan’s missiles struck. Arthur grabbed the railing, his knuckles white. Through the viewport, fire bloomed around Antares’ transports—orange against the storm-gray sky. “They’re here,” Garret muttered, slinging his shotgun over his shoulder. “Told you Titan wouldn’t wait.” Veyra stood frozen, her red lips parted. “This… wasn’t part of the plan.” “Nothing’s part of Titan’s plan,” Jaxon Cole said. The Titan enforcer materialized in the doorway, his buzzcut gleaming under the vessel’s flickering lights. A grenade pin spun between his fingers. “Arthur Neegan. You’re coming with us.” His voice was gravel, but his smile was electric. “Alive, if you’re smart.” Arthur stepped forward, Scout whining at his heels. “I’m not your damn cargo.” Jaxon tossed the grenade pin into the air. “Suit yourself.” The blast tore through the vessel’s hull. Chaos erupted. Alarms wailed as oxygen hissed into the void. Arthur lunged for the escape pod, Garret and Mira covering him. Veyra screamed, clutching her datapad as debris slammed into the wall. “Move!” Jaxon barked, firing at the pod’s controls. A bullet sparked off the alloy. Kael Renner emerged from the smoke, his cybernetic arm whirring as he reloaded. “Arthur! Titan’s offering a better deal!” “Bullshit,” Arthur hissed. “You’re Antares’ spy.” Kael’s human eye flickered. “Antares is dead. Titan’s paying *me* now.” He tossed Arthur a plasma cutter. “Cut the pod loose. *Now.*” Arthur stared at the tool. Betrayal stung sharper than the cold. But Scout was trapped in the corner, pinned under a collapsed console. He grabbed the cutter. “Wait!” Veyra lunged, her glove tearing on the debris. “The Azra vein—it’s unstable! If this ship breaks up—” “Azra’s *your* problem,” Jaxon sneered. The pod’s hatch opened. Arthur climbed in, Mira and Garret shoving him forward. “Go!” Garret yelled. The pod ejected, spinning wildly as Titan’s boarding team fired on it. Arthur’s hands trembled as he piloted them through the storm. Lightning flashed, illuminating the ruins of his homestead below—Antares’ drones already circling it. “Kael’s a traitor,” Mira said, bandaging a cut on her arm. “We can’t trust him.” “He saved our lives,” Arthur snapped. “For now.” Garret grunted. “For now.” The pod’s comms crackled. Veyra’s voice, frayed at the edges: “Arthur, listen to me—Azra’s decay chain is accelerating. If your pod impacts… Mars *will* blow.” Arthur’s blood turned to ice. “How long?” “Hours. Maybe less.” “Then we surface fast. Blow the vein before Titan or Antares does.” Mira shook her head. “You’re insane. The corp fleets will tear us apart.” “Better that than becoming their slave,” Arthur said. Outside, the storm raged. The pod plunged toward the crimson soil, Azra’s glow pulsing like a heartbeat in the distance. --- **Key Details:** - **Ambush tone:** Chaotic, sensory overload (smell of burning metal, alarms, debris). - **Character choices:** Arthur’s split loyalty, Kael’s betrayal, Veyra’s hidden fear of Azra. - **Stakes:** Homestead destroyed, timeline for Azra’s meltdown tightened. - **Foreshadowing:** Kael’s shifting allegiances, Veyra’s guilt hinted at through dialogue. - **Retro-tech:** Plasma cutters, analog comms, flickering holograms.

**Chapter 4: The Lab** The abandoned Antares lab was buried beneath a dead lava flow, its entrance hidden behind a bluff of rusted drones. Arthur kicked aside a skeletal exosuit, its joints frozen open like a spider’s legs. “Reminds me of the old salvage yards,” Garret said, kicking a cracked vial of Azra samples. The green liquid inside sloshed, glowing faintly. “Save the poetry,” Mira muttered, her flashlight cutting through the dust. “If this place blows, we won’t need verses.” Dr. Voss preceded them, her lab coat brushing against jagged rock. “The Azra’s decay chain is exponential. Every hour, the half-life shortens. If we don’t stabilize it—” “We blow Mars,” Arthur finished. The lab’s airlock hissed open. Inside, the walls hummed with fraying vacuum tubes. Holographic blueprints flickered above workbenches, casting ghostly light on rows of Azra crystals. The air smelled of ozone and decay. “Antares stripped the good equipment,” Voss said, gesturing to a rusted centrifuge. “But the core reactor’s intact. It’s the only way to slow the decay.” Arthur frowned. “You’ve used this before?” Voss’s hands shook as she calibrated a terminal. “Yes. At Titan. It… didn’t go well.” A hologram erupted—a cross-section of the Azra vein. Red pulses raced through it like infected blood. “See those nodes?” Voss pointed. “If we inject neutron absorbers here, we can slow the reaction. Buy time.” “Time for what?” Kael Renner asked, leaning in. His cybernetic eye whirred as he scanned the data. “To let Antares or Titan mine it anyway?” Voss didn’t answer. Her gaze lingered on a photo taped to the wall—a young woman with Arthur’s same stubborn jaw. *Her daughter*, Arthur realized. The one who died in the Titan accident. Garret broke the silence. “So we’re your backup plan? Fix your mess and hope it doesn’t kill us?” “Fixing it requires access to the vein,” Voss said. “Antares and Titan are already en route. You’re my only hope.” Arthur crossed his arms. “Why should I trust you?” Voss slid a datapad toward him. Images flashed: Titan’s warships, Antares’ enforcers, and a security feed of his homestead—*still burning*. “Because if I don’t stop this, neither will they.” --- The reactor chamber was a tomb of cracked concrete and sparking wires. Arthur climbed into the control pod, his helmet sealing with a hiss. Voss’s voice crackled over the comms. “The absorbers are loaded. Activate the sequence.” Green lights blinked on the console. Arthur’s fingers flew over the keys. “Wait.” Voss’s tone sharpened. “The absorbers are set to *disperse*. It won’t stop the decay—it’ll spread it.” Arthur froze. “You said—” “I said what I had to say to get you here.” Her voice broke. “Antares wants a controlled extraction. I want to *end* it. The absorbers will trigger a chain reaction in the vein. It’ll take hours, maybe days, but the decay will stabilize.” “A chain reaction,” Kael repeated. “You mean a *bomb*.” “Yes.” Arthur’s mind raced. Stabilize the Azra, or blow Mars to dust. “Do it.” The pod shuddered as the sequence initiated. Alarms blared. Somewhere in the chamber, a crystal shattered, spewing radiation. “Scout!” Arthur’s dog whined from the entrance, trapped by a collapsed beam. Voss’s laughter was hollow. “Priorities, Mr. Neegan.” “Get her out!” “I can’t—” “*Move!*” Arthur slammed his fist on the emergency release. The pod’s door slid open, and Garret lunged into the chamber, hauling Scout back just as a secondary explosion rocked the lab. --- Debris rained down. The hologram of the vein flickered, nodes turning dark. “It’s working,” Voss whispered. “The decay’s slowing…” “Liar,” Jaxon Cole said, materializing in the doorway. His grenade pin spun lazily. “You wanted a bomb. You got one.” Voss stepped forward, unflinching. “The reaction’s contained. Your ships’ll find a dead vein.” Jaxon’s smile didn’t reach his eyes. “Then we’ll dig.” Arthur grabbed a plasma torch from the wall. “You’ll dig in hell.” The lab’s lights died. In the dark, Arthur heard Voss’s voice—soft, desperate. “The absorbers… they’re still active. The reaction’s irreversible.” “Then we die trying,” Arthur said. Somewhere deep in the chamber, Azra glowed brighter. --- **Key Details:** - **Lab atmosphere:** Claustrophobic, retro-tech, littered with Antares/Titan remnants. - **Voss’s conflict:** Guilt over her daughter’s death drives her to sabotage both corps. - **Tension:** Radiation leaks, collapsing infrastructure, Jaxon’s betrayal. - **Foreshadowing:** The unstable absorbers hint at a larger sacrifice in Chapter 7. - **Arthur’s resolve:** His choice to save Scout (and Voss’s hidden agenda) solidifies his role as a reluctant leader.

**Chapter 5: The Double Agent** The lab’s flickering lights cast jagged shadows as Arthur slumped against a console, his hands still trembling from the reactor sequence. Scout curled at his feet, her fur matted with ash. Across the room, Dr. Voss stared at the dying hologram of the Azra vein, her shoulders slumped. Jaxon Cole leaned against the doorframe, grenade pin spinning lazily between his fingers. Kael Renner knelt in the corner, rewiring his cybernetic arm. His movements were too deliberate, his gaze too sharp. “Everything back to normal, then?” Arthur asked, nodding at Kael. The spy didn’t look up. “Wouldn’t want Antares’ drones sniffing around.” “Drones?” Voss snapped. “You’re repairing your comms?” Kael’s lips twitched. “Just fixing what’s broken.” Arthur’s gut tightened. *Too calm. Too rehearsed.* He rose, striding toward the corner. “Let me see.” Kael hesitated, then held out his arm. The prosthetic’s joints whirred as Arthur pried it open, revealing a nest of wires and a tiny Antares transmitter. “You’re leaking data,” Arthur said coldly. “I’m *surviving*,” Kael shot back. “Titan’s paying triple what Antares offered. And you?” He gestured to the crumbling lab. “You’re going to die here, Neegan. Blowing up your own vein to be a hero.” “Better than being their puppet,” Arthur spat. Kael’s human eye gleamed. “You think I wanted this? Titan took my brother. Left him in a refinery cell ‘til his lungs bled. Antares would’ve done the same. This?” He tossed the arm aside. “This is justice.” Arthur’s mind raced. *The tracker. The comms. Titan’s ships are already en route.* “You’re done,” he said. “We’re leaving.” Voss intervened, her voice sharp. “Stand down, Arthur. Kael’s information bought us time. Use it.” “Time for what?” Jaxon interrupted, stepping into the light. “To watch Mars burn?” He tossed a datapad to Arthur. “Antares just transmitted coordinates to every corp in the system. Your little lab bomb just made Azra the hottest commodity in the galaxy.” Arthur scrolled through the data. Dozens of fleets were rerouting toward the vein—Antares, Titan, even fringe warlords. *The countdown to the meltdown is irrelevant now. They’ll tear the planet apart fighting over it.* Kael laughed, a hollow sound. “Told you. You’re all dead men.” Mira emerged from the shadows, shotgun raised. “Save the drama. Antares’ enforcers are six klicks out. Titan’s got them in a crossfire.” Arthur’s crew turned on Kael, accusations flying. Voss moved to the comms, her tone smooth as poison. “Mr. Neegan. Let’s discuss *alternatives*.” The hologram flared, revealing Antares’ orbital station—a glittering fortress in the sky. “I can get you off Mars,” Veyra said. “In exchange, you hand over the lab’s coordinates. We’ll stabilize the Azra… *our* way.” Arthur’s jaw clenched. *Stable. Their way.* He thought of his homestead, Scout’s whimpers, Mira’s defiant glare. “And if I say no?” Veyra’s smile didn’t reach her eyes. “Then your crew dies here, and Azra’s secrets die with them. Titan’ll sell the vein’s location to the highest bidder. The corps always win, Arthur.” Kael snorted. “Newsflash—they’re already winning.” The lab’s alarms blared. Red lights pulsed as the reactor’s residual glow flickered outside. *The decay’s accelerating. Hours left.* Arthur studied Veyra, then Jaxon, then his crew. *Trust is a bullet you don’t waste.* “Fine,” he said to Veyra. “But I’m coming with you.” Mira’s shotgun lowered. “Like hell you are.” “You want to survive?” Arthur turned to her. “Then we play their game. But *we* control the terms.” Veyra’s datapad buzzed. “The station’s shuttle is ready. You have 20 minutes.” As the crew argued, Kael slipped a data chip from his pocket—a backup of the lab’s files. *For Titan. Or maybe for himself.* He pocketed it, watching Arthur’s face. *Hero or fool? Either way, I’ll be gone by the time the bombs fall.* --- **Key Details:** - **Kael’s Betrayal:** Revealed through subtle cues (rewiring comms, calm demeanor) before the confrontation. - **Veyra’s Manipulation:** Offers escape but maintains control, highlighting her ruthlessness. - **Stakes Escalate:** Fleets converging on Mars, Azra’s decay timeline compressed. - **Character Dynamics:** Arthur’s leadership tested; Mira’s loyalty wavers; Voss’s guilt peaks. - **Foreshadowing:** Kael’s data chip hints at his future double-cross; Veyra’s “control” phrase echoes in Chapter 7.
